<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_c3051731-7e9f-4e05-9100-dc9478b26539'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1134716458" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2048646885299625344">One of the most significant spiritual experience of my life has happened around this book. I had moved to Mumbai. Loneliness and depression were my accompanying emotions- 24*7. During those days, I didn't use to have money and I used to travel by buses. I was reading this book on the bus. The conductor gave me the ticket. He asked me how did I find the book? Now, this is not a title you expect to be read by the bus conductor. But he told me he has read the book. He started talking with me about </span>
  <span id="freeText2048646885299625344" style="display:none">One of the most significant spiritual experience of my life has happened around this book. I had moved to Mumbai. Loneliness and depression were my accompanying emotions- 24*7. During those days, I didn't use to have money and I used to travel by buses. I was reading this book on the bus. The conductor gave me the ticket. He asked me how did I find the book? Now, this is not a title you expect to be read by the bus conductor. But he told me he has read the book. He started talking with me about this book. I literally felt that there is someone out there who look out for each and every soul. I felt the connection with universe which is difficult to express in words. </span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3155351" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6777562343462105044">This book inspired by true story. To write this book, Dominique Lappier had to live in slum area in Calcutta, India for about two years. So that this slum area became the setting of the book.<br/><br/>The book told about the story of epic in a place called Anand Nagar or City of Joy. For Brother Stephan - a priest from Poland - came to this place made him to a suprise. He had to live in such filtry condition: a few water to share, bad sanition, etc. But, here, Stephan found the light of God that he seeked</span>
  <span id="freeText6777562343462105044" style="display:none">This book inspired by true story. To write this book, Dominique Lappier had to live in slum area in Calcutta, India for about two years. So that this slum area became the setting of the book.<br /><br />The book told about the story of epic in a place called Anand Nagar or City of Joy. For Brother Stephan - a priest from Poland - came to this place made him to a suprise. He had to live in such filtry condition: a few water to share, bad sanition, etc. But, here, Stephan found the light of God that he seeked for. All the people in Anand Nagar, eventhough lived in very poor condition, they care on another. Brother Stephan learned much things from the people in Anand Nagar. Here, people in all religion (Hindu, Budha, Moeslem, and Christian) lived in a high tolerance. <br /><br />For Hasari Pal, leaving his home in Bihar, Bengal was not what he wanted. The fall short of harvest, made him to sell his cow: Rani and left his home to Calcutta for better living. Then, he found that Calcutta was harder than in Bihar. He made his children became beggar, and Hasari himself had to sell his blood. They all lived in the street. By the help of a friend, Ram, Hasari became an pedicab driver. From the money he collected, Hasari could afford him and his family to move to Anand Nagar. Until one day, Hasari found himself was affected by TBC - many poor people like Hasari suffered from this illness, including his best friend, Ram - But Hasari still have one obligue, to find his eldest daughter a husband. But, finding a good husband meant expensive dowry. To complete the dowry, Hasari had to sell his (dead) body for medical purposes. Hasari died at the day his daughter wedding. <br /><br />Besides Hasari Pal and Brother Stephan, there were so many heroes in this book such as Marx, a doctor from America, Mother Sabia, etc. <br /><br />This book has been filmed in 1985 and starred by Patrick Swayze and Om Puri as Hasari. <br /><br />For me, this book enlighted me. I learned how to more appreciate to other people and any of living things.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6777562343462105044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1108283641?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anil Swarup" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anil Swarup" href="/user/show/8052004-anil-swarup">Anil Swarup</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1108283641" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer227050640044313411">It is one of the most amazing books one has ever come across. The pathos of chaos couldn't have been better described. The book does not celebrate poverty but the author paints an incredible picture of the denizens of this city of Joy : "The poorer they were, the more eager they were to give ". He goes to describe "the miracle of these concentration camps" where "the accumulation of disastrous elements were counter balanced by other factors that allowed their inhabitants not merely to remain ful</span>
  <span id="freeText227050640044313411" style="display:none">It is one of the most amazing books one has ever come across. The pathos of chaos couldn't have been better described. The book does not celebrate poverty but the author paints an incredible picture of the denizens of this city of Joy : "The poorer they were, the more eager they were to give ". He goes to describe "the miracle of these concentration camps" where "the accumulation of disastrous elements were counter balanced by other factors that allowed their inhabitants not merely to remain fully human but even to transcend their condition and become models of humanity "<br />He takes a dig at the bureaucracy while narrating his brush with the custom authorities calling the customs office as "classic shrine to bureaucracy "<br />What the city of Calcutta offers to the famished immigrant is " faint hope of finding some crumbs to allow them to survive until the next day"<br />The book has an interesting end: the poorest finding hope in the perceived empowerment in the nuclear tests. Ironical.<br />A must read for those that want to connect with the harsh ground realities that get outlined through some touching and poignant stories.</span>
  <a data-text-id="227050640044313411" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1163119511" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1163119511" />
    <a title="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all"><img alt="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1406361683p2/20738920.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1163119511?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" href="/user/show/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all">Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=thinking-people-s-books">thinking-people-s-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=couldn-t-put-it-down">couldn-t-put-it-down</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1163119511" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13322262802508747500">The person who lent me this book was not someone I consider a close friend. When I gave it back to her she asked me if I "enjoyed" it. Without thinking, I responded, "It's not the kind of book you "enjoy", it's the kind of book that changes your life." She didn't get it.<br/><br/>It certainly changed my perspective on a lot of things. Yes, it is a novel, but it's based on the author's experiences with Calcutta's poorest. For some odd reason, it made me very aware of just how dependent we are in the West o</span>
  <span id="freeText13322262802508747500" style="display:none">The person who lent me this book was not someone I consider a close friend. When I gave it back to her she asked me if I "enjoyed" it. Without thinking, I responded, "It's not the kind of book you "enjoy", it's the kind of book that changes your life." She didn't get it.<br /><br />It certainly changed my perspective on a lot of things. Yes, it is a novel, but it's based on the author's experiences with Calcutta's poorest. For some odd reason, it made me very aware of just how dependent we are in the West on electricity--and how we take it for granted. You plug stuff in, it works. You don't worry about it. But when the current isn't there, due to storm or breakdown or not paying the bill on time, <i>nothing</i> in the First World works anymore. Refrigeration. Computers. All that technology which has taken over our lives. Professionals now depend on their little electronic (electric) friends for absolutely everything. All that microsurgery etc. that they can do now--without electricity to run the computers and the lasers and the special technotools--impossible. And many of today's professionals don't learn alternate ways to do things, so they're helpless if there's no "juice" in the outlets.<br /><br />The family's story is more powerful <i>because</i> it is not unique. Untold millions, though they may not be rickshaw men, live on a knife's edge every single day. The story of "blood donors"--and of where many natural-bone skeletons actually come from--shook me to my core. And I don't use those words lightly.<br /><br />I later listened to M. La Pierre speak of his experiences in India, and though his manner in real life was a taste pompous, I put it down to being a gent of a certain age and cultural background--and perhaps discomfort at being interviewed live on TV.<br /><br />If you read this book, do so mindfully. Step back from emotional "oh isn't that terrible" and focus on the fact that <i>this is reality for hundreds of thousands of people every day, in our "modern" world.</i><br />Don't read it at all if you aren't going to let it touch you. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13322262802508747500" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_458329438" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/458329438" />
    <a title="Vivek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2830824-vivek"><img alt="Vivek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1380140012p2/2830824.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/458329438?book_show_action=true">Nov 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vivek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vivek" href="/user/show/2830824-vivek">Vivek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788176210522" href="/book/show/12398843-the-city-of-joy-dominique-lapierre">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer458329438" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15932546429347053548">I haven't read such a beautiful book in recent times. Not that the writing is all that good, but the content and the real stories of struggle,pain and the spirit to life that strives even in the worst of conditions makes you wonder about the sheer mental strength it takes to be one among the original "City of Joy".<br/><br/>Though the setting is quite similar to largely explored themes in Indian movies and books by now, the sheer intensity of involvement of the main characters with the many heroes of the </span>
  <span id="freeText15932546429347053548" style="display:none">I haven't read such a beautiful book in recent times. Not that the writing is all that good, but the content and the real stories of struggle,pain and the spirit to life that strives even in the worst of conditions makes you wonder about the sheer mental strength it takes to be one among the original "City of Joy".<br /><br />Though the setting is quite similar to largely explored themes in Indian movies and books by now, the sheer intensity of involvement of the main characters with the many heroes of the slum as well as the way each small detail is portrayed makes you feel each pang of pain that goes through the lives of these men and women destined to live a wretched life in the slums of the first major city of India.<br /><br />The author has taken pains to bring forth the logical reasons behind the stale and wretched state of these lives,while exploring the practical steps required to solve the problem by its roots. He also brings forth the stories of extreme care and love that is prevalent among the residents of this hellish slum,through the eye of a European. It transports you to an emerging India, more than half a decade ago, where the norms of equality and riches were limited to very few quarters. People were experiencing a major shift from the lifestyle of their ancestors, and the disasters brought about by the ruthless climate of the region coupled with the inability of the Government to handle disasters of such scales completely threw the lives of millions out of track.<br /><br />Such as it is, the author has also sprinkled in quite a lot of amusing incidents that provoke an involuntary smile in the reader's face amidst all the dashing images of people unfortunate enough to never breath some fresh air, or think of a clutter free, clean life. With their water polluted,bodies diseased,rights denied and brutally kept on the run by the urban machinery, death was sometimes more welcoming than the suffering. But the amount of life and hope displayed by the characters in this story is unbelievable. And remember that this is no fiction.<br /><br />This book is undoubtedly a masterpiece, not just of its time, but for ever, because it spreads the story and message that religions all over the world have failed to do. Love,kindness,hope and the willingness to strive for life. The way many a priests find their true God among the poor and suffering, this book reminds its readers that whatever men have been searching for all the centuries has always been within them. They just had to start acting on it. Just go and read it. I wouldn't want to write anymore about it than chew on its contents. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15932546429347053548" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12834341129907872190">I am not sure how to start in my review for this book. As a read it was written in a style that keeps you engaged. Alternating most chapters back and forth between two story lines allows you to become involved with the current and still be expectant about the coming chapter. Eventually both of these stories weave into one. Having just recently been to India I found the details to be accurate and he paints a viable image so you can see the story as you read it. <br/><br/>From a social standpoint, I was rep</span>
  <span id="freeText12834341129907872190" style="display:none">I am not sure how to start in my review for this book. As a read it was written in a style that keeps you engaged. Alternating most chapters back and forth between two story lines allows you to become involved with the current and still be expectant about the coming chapter. Eventually both of these stories weave into one. Having just recently been to India I found the details to be accurate and he paints a viable image so you can see the story as you read it. <br /><br />From a social standpoint, I was repeatedly overcome with such compassion and hurt for these people only to be touched and filled with joy that there are people like Brother Stephan and others who care so much about what is important in life and that is people. Reading how those with less than nothing give to others inspires me and reaffirms my hope in all of us. This book was as moving of a story as I have ever read. If all of the bad news of the world has disheartened you, read this book. There is nothing like love to lift you.</span>

<div id="review_1386464681" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1386464681" />
    <a title="Susanna Meynle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38980985-susanna-meynle"><img alt="Susanna Meynle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464495148p2/38980985.jpg" /></a>

      </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38980985-susanna-meynle?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38980985-susanna-meynle?shelf=will-read-again">will-read-again</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38980985-susanna-meynle?shelf=one-of-the-best-ever">one-of-the-best-ever</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38980985-susanna-meynle?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38980985-susanna-meynle?shelf=unputdownable">unputdownable</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1386464681" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1808484234335038516">Kolkata is the one place in India that I travel to often. One of the places I actually want to go to college to. <br/><br/>To say this book changed my life is too much of an understatement. This book warms my heart as much as it breaks my heart. <br/><br/>My dad is Indian but he's not from mainland India, so he belongs to 'Tribal India'. We stay in a small city which was popularly known in the old days (and until now) as "Scotland of the East" due to the fact that the weather is pretty pleasant here as compared to </span>
  <span id="freeText1808484234335038516" style="display:none">Kolkata is the one place in India that I travel to often. One of the places I actually want to go to college to. <br /><br />To say this book changed my life is too much of an understatement. This book warms my heart as much as it breaks my heart. <br /><br />My dad is Indian but he's not from mainland India, so he belongs to 'Tribal India'. We stay in a small city which was popularly known in the old days (and until now) as "Scotland of the East" due to the fact that the weather is pretty pleasant here as compared to other parts of India. Why I'm adding this, is to shamefully share that though I am half Indian I have no idea whatsoever about the life of the greater population of India- the farmers, the poor, the peasants. <br /><br />When I was in Kolkata as a kid with my parents, more than once the ignorant thought crossed my mind when I saw homeless people on the streets, "Why do they even come and live in the city anyway when they can easily farm in the countryside?" Sure, I knew about the droughts and famine that hits India time and again, but doesn't the government provide enough irrigation and other relief supplies? Well the answer is No. <br /><br />This book primarily follows the story of the oldest son in a family who is forced to leave his ancestral, drought ridden village, in search of a better job in the city of Calcutta. He takes along with him his wife and children. The author takes us along in the protagonist's search for any job that comes his way, as well as any means he can get to provide for his family. Sacrificing himself along the way.<br /><br />Then we follow a Polish priest who comes to live in the City of Joy. He lives among the poorest and the shunned, learning their ways and trying to let them accept him.<br /><br />The story includes the community of the leprous, who are secluded in one part of the slum area. It includes a woman who is desperate for money to feed her kids- desperate enough to sacrifice one on the way. It includes a society that comes to love the 'white man' living among them as one of their own. It includes the complicated intricacies of the old Hindu marriage. It includes people, people like you and me.<br /> <br />It is only when I read this book that it hit me- they too have a story, they too have dreams, hopes and aspirations for the future. <br /><br />it is only after I read this book that I realized how much harder life is for them. No, they're not happy to beg and send their children out to look for scraps, they have no choice. <br /><br />It is only when I read this book that my eyes were opened about how twisted and complicated the system really is. How it isn't as simple as it looks. Just how wretched our society is. And yet, in the midst of all this twisted, wretched and complicated system- that you can still find peace, hope, satisfaction, happiness and most importantly: Joy, in the smallest things of life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1808484234335038516" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1649393807" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1649393807" />
    <a title="Sethu Jose" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30589252-sethu-jose"><img alt="Sethu Jose" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1489201639p2/30589252.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1649393807?book_show_action=true">May 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sethu Jose" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sethu Jose" href="/user/show/30589252-sethu-jose">Sethu Jose</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780446323864" href="/book/show/1813920.The_City_of_Joy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1649393807" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10636077129315992161"><i>Read books to live those lives that you could never have.</i><br/><p>The City of Joy is a book that would plunge you into a vortex of bare reality filled with misery, abjection, hope and love. Finally, when you emerge from this whirlwind of experiences, your view on life would never be the same.</p><p>When I picked up this book to get a relief from the bunch of overwhelmingly high soaring books which I had been reading recently, little did I know that it was a cornucopia of humanity which would leave an everlastin</p></span>
  <span id="freeText10636077129315992161" style="display:none"><i>Read books to live those lives that you could never have.</i><br /><p>The City of Joy is a book that would plunge you into a vortex of bare reality filled with misery, abjection, hope and love. Finally, when you emerge from this whirlwind of experiences, your view on life would never be the same.</p><p>When I picked up this book to get a relief from the bunch of overwhelmingly high soaring books which I had been reading recently, little did I know that it was a cornucopia of humanity which would leave an everlasting impression on my consciousness and that humans are capable of being united even in the midst of wretched misery.</p><p>The pages of this book are not exuding just any story, but the life stories of many Mr. Lapierrie got the chance to interview among the destitute inhabitants of the dying city of Calcutta. Set in post colonial India, this book elucidates the misery of the poor and sick, which the general populace have conveniently learnt to overlook by habituation.</p><p>Stephen Kovalski, the priest with a relentless obsession to serve the poor, should be beatified (if fictional saints exist!) for being impeccably altruistic, showering his love on the helpless in the City of Joy.</p><p>
  <i>"It was a drop of water in the ocean of needs, but a drop of water that would be missed if it were not there"</i>
</p><p>Lapierre has portrayed Hasari Pal as the befitting example of a Bengal peasant, driven to destitution by natural calamities. His efforts to make ends meet, in a society abound with religious beliefs and superstitions, will definitely melt even the hardest of the hearts. The real horror of his life unfolds when an account of human-bone market in Calcutta is elucidated with at most mediocrity. On the other end, Max Loeb, a good-hearted American doctor who flew over to Calcutta only to be overwhelmed by the circus of suffering in the City of Joy, would overpower his personal fears to fit in where he is most needed. Lapierre managed to bring out the beauty and irony of life through these two characters - Hasari and Max. For one misery was inevitable, for the other it was a choice.</p><i>"The gods have not made it easy to be a human being"</i>, Hasari would say to which Max replies, <i>"No they haven't. But I guess that's why it feels so goddamn wonderful to beat the odds."</i><p>The book celebrates the tranquilizing notion that it is in the midst of poverty and helplessness that wholehearted love and care could be found. The story revolves around the life of many who make us realize how futile our life's problems are. They set an example to the world that it is high time we overlook venomous pangs of social stigmas and enmities, and embark upon a reformation to re-institute a society which centralizes on love and solidarity.</p><p>
  <i>"Misfortune is great, but man is even greater than misfortune."</i>
</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="10636077129315992161" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer698790649" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14413551507462315100">One of the best books I've read so far. Brings the reader face to face with the the people who lived in the Calcutta slums - their struggles , their pain , their joy and their magic. Dominique Lapierre writes a total unbiased account of the the people living in the new born independent India. His narrative transports you to the city of joy where people are the happiest ,despite living in the worst possible conditions. A learning lesson , indeed.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1805885617" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1805885617" />
    <a title="Zineb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61327034-zineb"><img alt="Zineb" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

<div id="review_926694483" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/926694483" />
    <a title="Tucker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/308912-tucker"><img alt="Tucker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582733342p2/308912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/926694483?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tucker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tucker" href="/user/show/308912-tucker">Tucker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385189521" href="/book/show/1474616.The_City_of_Joy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/308912-tucker?shelf=existential">existential</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/308912-tucker?shelf=idea-list-eunuchs-in-nonfiction">idea-list-eunuchs-in-nonfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer926694483" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14409185380646344969">An exposé of poverty in wretched detail. People die slowly from backbreaking work or from hunger, or their limbs melt from leprosy long after their families have banished them because of the disease, or they fall into festering sewers and drown unnoticed. The slums of Calcutta arose due to an influx of people who exchanged the deprivation of the rural life for urban poverty, which was just another kind of precarious existence: "The earthquake that shook Bihar on January 15, 1937, caused hundreds</span>
  <span id="freeText14409185380646344969" style="display:none">An exposé of poverty in wretched detail. People die slowly from backbreaking work or from hunger, or their limbs melt from leprosy long after their families have banished them because of the disease, or they fall into festering sewers and drown unnoticed. The slums of Calcutta arose due to an influx of people who exchanged the deprivation of the rural life for urban poverty, which was just another kind of precarious existence: "The earthquake that shook Bihar on January 15, 1937, caused hundreds of thousands of deaths and catapulted entire villages in the direction of Calcutta."<br /><br />This story follows a Polish Catholic priest who voluntarily lives among the poor. No matter how spare his living conditions, he is perceived as rich, because he is sometimes able to make food and medicine appear in emergencies, whereas the truly poor among the Indians did not have such power.<br /><br />This is a seemingly unending epic, without a plot or a strong narrative drive. It was based on the author's experiences and research, features real people, and is essentially a non-fiction collection of vignettes. Lapierre and his wife founded the organization Action Aid for Lepers' Children of Calcutta; today it is called <a href="http://cityofjoyaid.org/" rel="nofollow noopener">City of Joy Aid</a>.<br /><br />The eunuchs appear three-quarters of the way through this very long book. The named eunuch is Kalima, after the goddess of destruction. No surprises there; the author mostly gives a straightforward explanation of the <i>hijra</i> and their social role.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14409185380646344969" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1399752963" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1399752963" />
    <a title="Jessica Culhane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39090265-jessica-culhane"><img alt="Jessica Culhane" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1399752963?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica Culhane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica Culhane" href="/user/show/39090265-jessica-culhane">Jessica Culhane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1399752963" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15643587905885919481">I read The City of Joy almost twenty years ago, in middle school. It had a profound effect on me, so when I saw a copy of it a few months ago in a book store, I bought it immediately. The second reading did not disappoint.<br/><br/>Much of the scenery of 1960s Calcutta was familiar to me as a tourist earlier this year to an entirely different part of India: the religious cacophony, the slum labor, the baksheesh, the festivals, the filth. Wild traffic, marigolds, eunuchs, kurtas and saris. Truly Kafkaesque</span>
  <span id="freeText15643587905885919481" style="display:none">I read The City of Joy almost twenty years ago, in middle school. It had a profound effect on me, so when I saw a copy of it a few months ago in a book store, I bought it immediately. The second reading did not disappoint.<br /><br />Much of the scenery of 1960s Calcutta was familiar to me as a tourist earlier this year to an entirely different part of India: the religious cacophony, the slum labor, the baksheesh, the festivals, the filth. Wild traffic, marigolds, eunuchs, kurtas and saris. Truly Kafkaesque bureaucracy. It seems that little has changed in the last fifty years, except that the rickshaws are now auto-rickshaws, and the cases of leprosy have been greatly reduced, with a drug cure widely disseminated for free in the past twenty years.<br /><br />Of course, much of India remains a mystery to tourists. Weddings, funerals, the monsoon, extreme poverty, superstitions, desperation. Blood, bone, and fetus buyers. Most of all, the intense human connections that the residents of Calcutta, and especially the City of Joy, have with each other. As the author states near the end, "Except perhaps for a trip to the moon, a stay in an Indian slum was the most extraordinary adventure a man of the twentieth century could live through."</span>
  <a data-text-id="15643587905885919481" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1804765588" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1804765588" />
    <a title="Khoo Zi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61285059-khoo-zi"><img alt="Khoo Zi" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1804765588?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Khoo Zi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Khoo Zi" href="/user/show/61285059-khoo-zi">Khoo Zi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1804765588" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11866256196779565461">Dominique LaPierre’s City of Joy celebrates the resilience and courage of the slum dwellers as they struggle against the dire and inhumane conditions in the city of Calcutta. LaPierre shares a story of hope, humanism and joy in the marginalized community that is often shunned and despised by the upper tiers of society. This provides us a fresh perspective to the common misrepresentation of the poor as despondent and miserable individuals, but instead are admired for their strength and will power</span>
  <span id="freeText11866256196779565461" style="display:none">Dominique LaPierre’s City of Joy celebrates the resilience and courage of the slum dwellers as they struggle against the dire and inhumane conditions in the city of Calcutta. LaPierre shares a story of hope, humanism and joy in the marginalized community that is often shunned and despised by the upper tiers of society. This provides us a fresh perspective to the common misrepresentation of the poor as despondent and miserable individuals, but instead are admired for their strength and will power. The City of Joy was an enlightening read as it highlights the positivity and resilience of the poor that we do not hear of usually. It tells a story of humanity and faith amidst the cruelty and struggles faced, and really opens my perception towards life and the people around me. <br /><br />Truly a beautiful read that highlights the power of life, love, and the people. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11866256196779565461" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2181908807" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer402894963636816504">When I first laid hands on this book, I had high expectations of it, having heard of it from my associates before. The read throughout had been an enjoyable one from start to finish, with its hauntingly beautiful descriptions and vivid narratives, allowing me to immerse myself fully into the story. There were three main narratives in the book each following the lives of three different people. Hasari Pan, a poor peasant from the countryside of Bengal driven to Calcutta due to an unforgiving drou</span>
  <span id="freeText402894963636816504" style="display:none">When I first laid hands on this book, I had high expectations of it, having heard of it from my associates before. The read throughout had been an enjoyable one from start to finish, with its hauntingly beautiful descriptions and vivid narratives, allowing me to immerse myself fully into the story. There were three main narratives in the book each following the lives of three different people. Hasari Pan, a poor peasant from the countryside of Bengal driven to Calcutta due to an unforgiving drought, Stephan Kovalski, a Polish Catholic priest of French nationality convicted by his faith to live among the poor in Anand Nagar, and Max Loeb, a skilled physician from America with a wealthy background joining Stephan Kovalski to provide healthcare to the poor in Anand Nagar. Three very different perspective and situations, yet they come together beautifully to display the beauty of Calcutta slums, of the great spirit that is held within the community of Anand Nagar, of the unwavering joy for life among those that hold so little possession. In each of their stories, there are many lessons that can be learned, lessons on how their actions affected the community, and how their leadership led to great or terrible things.<br /><br />The most obvious lesson can be taken from Stephan Kovalski, for without a doubt he was a leader of a small part of the community in Anand Nagar, by virtue of the colour of his skin and the respect he commanded as a holy man in the community’s eye. He came into the community of Anand Nagar, to live and breathe among the poor, to be like one of them, yet in this regard, he had failed in achieving this. It was only natural for him to be elevated to a position of leadership, for he was a holy man and Indians held holy men in high regards. He had not wanted it, being convicted by faith to live among them, not to lead them. Yet when faced with that responsibility, he accepted it as his duty, serving the people not with pride and power but with his gentle humility, truly striving to make the community a better place. It’s through his action that this thought dominated my mind while reading his narrative, that great community leaders are not those that seek the position of authority. Rather, they are leaders chosen by the people through their actions, who accept the position not out of a sense of entitlement but rather a sense of duty to the community, and thereafter serve the community with humility striving to better others.<br /><br />However, far from being a perfect leader of the community, Stephan Kovalski was flawed too, as any human should be despite their position of authority. No doubt he seeks the best for the people in his community, opening a night school for the children, setting up a Committee of Mutual Aid, and introducing a leper clinic. Yet in a moment of pride or angry, I cannot say, he chose to defy the orders of the local mafia boss, a man of substantial political and financial influence. In a way, I understand that the exorbitant fee demanded by the local mafia boss for Kovalski to set up a leper clinic in the middle of Anand Nagar was unreasonable, but the power the mafia boss held was significant and he would have made life hard not only for Kovalski, but also the residents of the community he was leading. This part of the story reminded me of one line in Game of Thrones used to describe a king too proud to surrender, ‘How many of his people died for his pride.’ Thus, I draw the second lesson as a community leader from this, that a leader should not put his own personal gain or feelings above that of his people, that he has been entrusted with a position of authority and his actions will have a great impact on his people, which could have great or terrible consequences.<br />From the story of Max Loeb, we too can learn another lesson about community leadership. Here was a man who was living in the lap of luxury, his dad a highly respected respiratory surgeon, and he himself a well-trained physician with a bright future laid out for him. Yet he chose to take a year off from his internship to serve the people in Anand Nagar with his skills in healthcare, choosing to leave behind his family and loving fiancée to do so. There he faced great trials, being completely out of his comfort zone due to the abysmal hygiene and lack of medical supplies, but he persevered on to serve the people there, earning the respect of the community in Anand Nagar. It is here I see that one does not have to be put in a position of authority to be a community leader. Simply by serving the community with a heart for them goes a long way, and Max Loeb himself was a community leader, despite only going there to serve.<br /><br />Lastly, the poor rickshaw rider Hasari Pal himself displayed leadership too, perhaps not in the community but definitely in his family. As the head of his household, he took it upon himself to look after his family, sacrificing his well-being for the sake of his family. Especially at the end where he scrounges together two thousand rupees as a dowry for his daughter, the dedication to his family was clear for all to see. And this is what I feel is sorely lacking in our society today, that reliable fathers are slowly disappearing from traditional families and many children are left floundering without a fatherly figure growing up, and many values are not passed down to the next generation.<br /><br />City of Joy was a long read, it took me about a week to finish it. Yet it was a pleasure from start to finish, and I truly enjoyed the journey, the lessons gleamed from the narratives provoked many thoughts from me and the vivid description of one of India’s slum, especially as having visited a slum in India before myself, was itself interesting. I would recommend this book to anyone who wish to see the strength of the human spirit even in times of extreme hardship.</span>
  <a data-text-id="402894963636816504" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText3956012431050620122" style="display:none">Dominique Lapierre vividly portrays the poverty, suffering, and destitution that is rife in the city. Yet in the midst of the sufferings, he celebrates the strength and unity of humanity.<br /><br />Lapierre also shed light on the concept of community leadership as he wrote about Stephan Kovalski, a priest with the heart to serve the cause of the poor. Kolvaski managed to lead the poor because he gain their trust and respect with his sincerity to experience what the poor are experiencing, and to live with the poor. As a result, he is able to work with the poor to improve their own lot.<br /><br />Overall, this book is a great read as it gives us real insights into the lives of the poorest of the poor, celebrates the strength of the humanity, and showed us what community leadership is about. </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('EYJA8QZgQYnRY14jIYShswKGtfa+qxLMeBu/WoQBRxO4pY75z4nsP09Dz/jTDAfc8Q7bZntI6Lex4EjXBJo9nQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('y3qUOyJmFzIGxP9Zh9tpkpYyZ9c1TeQ9uoXPoAIGgFRiXVoz64+6hJjkboJ1U8/9ZboJR/CuHkZzfjgtgp362g==')})
  }

I began to read, fascinated. Here was the whole chronicle illustrated with anecdotes and masterful character sketches of how the British had come to India, how they had ruled it and how, finally, compelled by the force of economics and history, they had been forced to leave it divided…… Collins and Lapierre are such good writers that their books are so interesting that they are impossible to put down.”\n—J.M. Shantaram is narrated by Lin, an escaped convict with a false passport who flees maximum security prison in Australia for the teeming streets of a city where he can disappear.\n\nAccompanied by his guide and faithful friend, Prabaker, the two enter Bombay\'s hidden society of beggars and gangsters, prostitutes and holy men, soldiers and actors, and Indians and exiles from other countries, who seek in this remarkable place what they cannot find elsewhere.\n\nAs a hunted man without a home, family, or identity, Lin searches for love and meaning while running a clinic in one of the city\'s poorest slums, and serving his apprenticeship in the dark arts of the Bombay mafia. The search leads him to war, prison torture, murder, and a series of enigmatic and bloody betrayals. The keys to unlock the mysteries and intrigues that bind Lin are held by two people. The first is Khader Khan: mafia godfather, criminal-philosopher-saint, and mentor to Lin in the underworld of the Golden City. The second is Karla: elusive, dangerous, and beautiful, whose passions are driven by secrets that torment her and yet give her a terrible power.\n\nBurning slums and five-star hotels, romantic love and prison agonies, criminal wars and Bollywood films, spiritual gurus and mujaheddin guerrillas – this huge novel has the world of human experience in its reach, and a passionate love for India at its heart. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12622708620226112666">Dominique Lapierre was born in Châtelaillon-Plage, Charente-Maritime, France. At the age of thirteen, he travelled to America with his father who was a diplomat (Consul General of France). He attended the Jesuit school in New Orleans and became a paper boy for the New Orleans Item. He developed interests in travelling, writing and cars.<br/><br/>He was awarded the Padma Bhushan, India's third highest civili</span>
  <span id="freeText12622708620226112666" style="display:none">Dominique Lapierre was born in Châtelaillon-Plage, Charente-Maritime, France. At the age of thirteen, he travelled to America with his father who was a diplomat (Consul General of France). He attended the Jesuit school in New Orleans and became a paper boy for the New Orleans Item. He developed interests in travelling, writing and cars.<br /><br />He was awarded the Padma Bhushan, India's third highest civilian award in the 2008 Republic Day honours list.</span>
